p. 121. The first use I have found in English is in John Perceval, Earl of Egmont, [3mA Full and Fair Discussion of the Pretensions 
of the Dissenters, to the Repeal of the Sacramental Test [0m(London, 1733), p. 14. It also appears in the 1773 โpoetical epistleโ [3mThe 
Dying Negro, [0mand in an early tract by the abolitionist leader Granville Sharp, [3mA Declaration of the Peopleโs Natural Right to a 
Share in the Legislature โฆ [0m(London, 1774), p. xxv. I found these using the online service of Thomson Gale, Eighteenth-Century 
Collections Online, and am grateful to Jenna Gibbs-Boyer for help with this research. Quote from Condorcet in [3mOeuvres complรจtes de 
Condorcet, [0med. by Maire Louise Sophie de Grouchy, marquise de Condorcet, 21 vols. (Brunswick: Vieweg; Paris: Henrichs, 1804), vol. 
XI, pp. 240โ42, 251, 249. Sieyรจs used the term [3mdroits de lโhomme [0monly once: โIl ne faut point juger de ses [Third Estateโs] 
demandes par les observations isolรฉes de quelques auteurs plus ou moins instruits des droits de lโhommeโโEmmanuel Sieyes, [3mLe 
Tiers-Etat [0m(1789; Paris: E. Champion, 1888), p. 36. In his letter to James Madison from Paris dated January 12, 1789, Thomas 
Jefferson sent Madison Lafayetteโs draft declaration. Its second paragraph began, โLes droits de lโhomme assurent sa proprietรฉ, sa 
libertรฉ, son honneur, sa vieโโ [3mJefferson Papers, [0mvol. 14, p. 438. Condorcetโs draft is dated to some time prior to the opening of 
the Estates-General on May 5, 1789, in Iain McLean and Fiona Hewitt, [3mCondorcet: Foundations of Social Choice and Political Theory [0m
(Aldershot, Hants: Edward Elgar, 1994), p. 57, and see pp. 255โ70 for the draft declaration โof rights,โ which uses the expression 
โrights of manโ but not in its title. See the texts of the various projects for a declaration in Antoine de Baecque, ed., [3mLโAn I

(13)

(14) 

J. B. Schneewind, [3mThe Invention of Autonomy: A History of Modern Moral Philosophy [0m(Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1998), 
p. 4. Autonomy seems to be the crucial element lacking in natural law theories up to the middle of the eighteenth century. As 
Haakonssen argues, โAccording to most natural lawyers in the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries, moral agency consisted in being 
subject to natural law and carrying out the duties imposed by such law, whereas rights were derivative, being mere means to the 
fulfilment of dutiesโโKnud Haakonssen, [3mNatural Law and Moral Philosophy: From Grotius to the Scottish Enlightenment [0m(Cambridge: 
Cambridge Univerity Press, 1996), p. 6. In this regard, Burlamaqui, such a great influence on the Americans in the 1760s and 
1770s, may well mark an important transition. Burlamaqui insists that men are subject to a superior power, but that that power 
must accord with manโs inner nature: โIn order for a law to regulate human actions, it must absolutely accord with the nature and 
the constitution of man and it must relate in the end to his happiness, which is what reason necessarily makes him seek 
outโโBurlamaqui, [3mPrincipes, [0mp. 89. On the general importance of autonomy to human rights, see Charles Taylor, [3mSources of the Self: 
The Making of Modern Identity [0m(Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1989), esp. p. 12.

Canning to show how English justice functioned in a superior manner but most of the pamphlet is devoted to the Calas case.

de la mort de Jean Calas [0m(1763). The quote is taken from Jacques van den Heuvel, ed., [3mMรฉlanges/Voltaire [0m(Paris: Gallimard, 1961), 
p. 583.

(5) 

On the general establishment and abolition of torture in Europe, see Edward Peters, [3mTorture [0m(Philadelphia: University of 
Pennsylvania Press, 1985). Although torture was not abolished in some Swiss cantons until the mid-nineteenth century, the practice 
largely disappeared (at least as legally recognized) in Europe in the course of the Revolutionary and Napoleonic wars. Napoleon 
abolished it in Spain, for example, in 1808, and it was never reestablished. For the history of the development of juries, see Sir 
James Fitzjames Stephen, [3mA History of the Criminal Law of England, [0m3 vols. (1883; Chippenham, Wilts: Routledge, 1996), vol. 1, pp. 
250โ54. On witchcraft cases and the use of torture, see Alan Macfarlane, [3mWitchcraft in Tudor and Stuart England: A Regional and 
Comparative Study [0m(London: Routledge & Kegan Paul, 1970), pp. 139-40; and Christina A. Larner, [3mEnemies of God: The Witch-hunt in 
Scotland [0m(London: Chatto & Windus, 1981), p. 109. As Larner points out, the constant injunctions from Scottish and English judges 
demanding an end to torture in witchcraft cases show that it remained an issue. James Heath, [3mTorture and English Law: An 
Administrative and Legal History from the Plantagenets to the Stuarts [0m(Westport, CT: Greenwood Press, 1982), p. 179, details 
several references to the use of the rack in the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries, but these were not sanctioned by common law. 
See also Kathryn Preyer, โPenal Measures in the American Colonies: An Overview,โ [3mAmerican Journal of Legal History, [0m26 (October 
1982): 326โ53, esp. p. 333. 

(6) 

On the general methods of punishment, see J. A. Sharpe, [3mJudicial Punishment in England [0m(London: Faber & Faber, 1990). Punishment 
on the pillory could include having oneโs ears cut off or having oneโs ear nailed to the pillory (p. 21). Stocks were a wooden 
device to hold the feet of an offender. The pillory was a device in which offenders stood with their head and hands between two 
pieces of woodโLeon Radzinowicz, [3mA History of English Criminal Law and Its Administration from 1750, [0m4 vols. (London: Stevens & 
Sons, 1948), vol. I, pp. 3โ5, and 165โ227. For a review of recent research in this now very richly mined vein, see Joanna Innes 
and John Styles, โThe Crime Wave: Recent Writing on Crime and Criminal Justice in Eighteenth-Century England,โ [3mJournal of British 
Studies, [0m25 (October 1986): 380โ435.

The Dutch scholar Peter Spierenburg traces moderation of punishment to growing empathy: โThe death and suffering of fellow human 
beings were increasingly experienced as painful, just because other people were increasingly perceived as fellow human 
beingsโโSpierenburg, [3mThe Spectacle of Suffering: Executions and the Evolution of Repression: From a Preindustrial Metropolis to 
the European Experience [0m(Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1984), p. 185. Beccaria, [3mCrimes and Punishments, [0mquotes pp. 43, 
107, and 112. Blackstone too argued for punishments proportional to crimes, and he lamented the large number of death penalty 
offenses in EnglandโWilliam Blackstone, [3mCommentaries on the Laws of England, [0m4 vols., 8th edn. (Oxford: Clarendon Press, 1778), 
vol. IV, p. 3. Blackstone cites Montesquieu and Beccaria in a note to this page. For the influence of Beccaria on Blackstone, see 
Coleman Phillipson, [3mTrue Criminal Law Reformers: Beccaria, Bentham, Romilly [0m(Montclair, NJ: Patterson Smith, 1970), esp. p. 90.

(4) 

I have not focused here on the distinction between natural law and natural rights, partly because in French-language works, such 
as Burlamaquiโs, it is often blurred. Moreover, eighteenth-century political figures did not necessarily make clear distinctions 
themselves. Burlamaquiโs 1747 treatise was translated immediately into English as [3mThe Principles of Natural Law [0m(1748) and then 
Dutch (1750), Danish (1757), Italian (1780), and eventually Spanish (1825)โBernard Gagnebin, [3mBurlamaqui et le droit naturel [0m
(Geneva: Editions de la Fregate, 1944), p. 227. Gagnebin claims that Burlamaqui had less influence in France, but one of the 
prominent authors writing for the [3mEncyclopรฉdic [0m(Boucher dโArgis) used him as his source for one of the articles on natural law. 
For Burlamaquiโs views on reason, human nature, and Scottish philosophy, see J. J. Burlamaqui, [3mPrincipes du droit naturel par J. 
J. Burlamaqui, Conseiller dโEtat, et cidevant Professeur en droit naturel et civil ร Genรจve [0m(Geneva: Barrillot et fils, 1747), pp. 
1-2 and 165.

(Paris: Debure lโaรฎnรฉ, 1752). T. Rutherforth, D.D. F.R.S., [3mInstitutes of Natural Law Being the substance of a Course of Lectures
